## Reply 312 — participant_228 · 2026-04-08 · post-31219866

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> lachek said: Hello everyone, and welcome to a very special dev diary! Today we finally get to pull back the curtain on what we're cooking up next for Crusader Kings III. As some of you may have already guessed, it's all about the very thing that made medieval Europe tick: the Christian Church. We're deep in development on this one and have a lot of ground still to cover, so this will be a broad overview of what we're building. Future dev diaries will go much deeper into individual systems, and we will be paying close attention to your feedback throughout. Before I get into all the juicy details, let me say a few words about what this expansion is and what it isn't, because I know the speculation machine has been running at full tilt. This is a systemic expansion focused on Christianity - with an emphasis on Catholicism - and the relationship between secular and ecclesiastical power. If you play anywhere in Christian Europe, the Byzantine Empire, or indeed anywhere Christianity has a presence, this expansion will transform the way you engage with your faith and the institutions which claim to speak for God. If you play outside of these regions you will still notice aspects of the revisions we're making to Faiths, but our aim is to add depth and immersive detail to Christian Europe first and foremost. With that out of the way, let's dig in. The Vision​If there is one sentence that captures what we're going for, it's this: the right to rule flows directly from God. In the medieval world, kings and emperors didn't just need armies and gold - they required the blessing of the Church. Your legitimacy as a ruler was inseparable from your standing as a Christian. The Pope could make or break dynasties. Bishops accumulated enormous wealth and wielded it as political leverage. Holy men preached ideas that could ignite revolutions. And through all of this, the Church itself was evolving - splitting, reforming, debating, and occasionally tearing itself apart over questions of theology that had very real consequences for the people caught up in them. We want you to feel all of that in the game. Whether you're a French king trying to get a favorable Pope elected, a minor count whose Archbishop is agitating for your excommunication, an ambitious duke trying to get yourself a Cardinal's hat, or the Pope himself trying to hold Christendom together while half your clergy are preaching things that would make Augustine weep, faith should be something you are constantly, meaningfully engaging with. Not a passive stat on your character sheet, but a living, breathing force that shapes your realm and your dynasty's future. View attachment 1480425 Faith Gets Personal​Let's start with the most fundamental change: how your character relates to their faith. Currently in CK3, your faith evokes a certain flavor, influences many game mechanics, and imposes certain modifiers. If you start as a Catholic, you have some doctrines and core tenets, certain traits are sinful or virtuous, and that's that until you convert or start your own faith. You don't really practice your faith in any meaningful way, nor does it evolve with the centuries. This is what we are looking to change. Spiritual Fulfillment is a new character metric that tracks how internally fulfilled your character is in their religious practice. Think of it as the spiritual counterpart to Stress, except instead of measuring how close you are to a mental breakdown, it measures how close you are to either sainthood or damnation. Your Spiritual Fulfillment is influenced by which religious practices (Tenets) you personally adopt as convictions, how well those align with your personality traits and lifestyle, the choices you make in events, and generally how closely your actions correspond with the beliefs of right practice in your faith. A Generous character who practices Asceticism and goes on regular pilgrimages will find deep spiritual peace. A Lustful character targeted by unceasing Seduction schemes will... have a harder time of it. Choosing the virtuous path despite your sinful nature is itself rewarded, even as it causes you Stress. Creating room for personal dilemmas to naturally emerge that players have a genuinely difficult time resolving is the kind of stuff us sadistic game designers live for. Tiers of Fulfillment - both positive and negative - unlock special decisions, modifiers, and events. High Fulfillment characters will be given temptations to overcome but have the ability to help others deal with spiritual conflicts, while those with low Fulfillment can try to repent. But even low Fulfillment comes with benefits, such as the ability to interfere with the Church more readily. View attachment 1480426 Tenet Revision​To make Spiritual Fulfillment work - and to make the whole religious system more dynamic - we're also doing a comprehensive revision of Tenets. We are making many new ones, and also reimagining existing ones, as active observances - things your character can choose to practice, champion, or secretly indulge in. Every Tenet will have both a faith-wide effect and a personal effect when a character adopts it into their daily practice, and Tenets can have different statuses within a faith — from Core teachings that define it, through approved and tolerated practices, all the way to outright forbidden ones. Adopting a forbidden Tenet means practicing it in secret, which of course means anyone who discovers your little theological indiscretion could get a juicy Secret Hook on you. This changes the entire dynamic of how faiths evolve. Instead of static labels, you get a living ecosystem of religious practice where ideas spread from person to person, where your Court Chaplain's own convictions actually matter, and where a new Pope's choice of which Tenets to elevate as Core teachings sends ripples across all of Christendom. Your character's spiritual journey could be as strategically important as their martial prowess or diplomatic cunning, and the theological preferences of the next Pope could make your carefully cultivated piety suddenly look a lot like heresy. Rites​One of the biggest mechanical additions is Rites, subdivisions of a faith by both character and geography that represent local variations in religious practice. In 1066, not every Catholic in Europe practiced their faith identically. In fact, the entire premise of Catholic (=universal) Christianity was to permit local variations of liturgy, as long as some basic theological premises were adhered to. The insular Irish emphasized different aspects of their faith than Mozarabic practitioners, neither of which perfectly adhered to Roman liturgy. These weren't heresies, but natural variations within a single faith, led by local archbishops who had their own theological priorities. Clergy characters can start new Rites in which they can make changes to which Tenets are emphasized. An archbishop might elevate one Approved Tenet to Core status while de-emphasizing another. As long as these changes are modest, Rome won't bat an eye. But if a Rite starts teaching practices the Pope considers forbidden, or accumulates too much Divergence from the mainline rite... well, that's how you get Cathars. And nobody wants Cathars. (Or do you? More on that later.) Rites spread along Ecclesiastical Title borders - a new geographic layer representing the ancient archdiocese regions - and characters within a Rite's domain can choose to adopt it. Over time, neighboring archdioceses may adopt the same Rite, creating regional blocs of distinct religious practice within Catholicism. This naturally soft-contains Rites within realm borders while still permitting the kind of organic drift and cross-pollination that made medieval Christianity so fascinatingly messy. The intended dynamic is that Rites evolve slowly, gradually drifting closer to or further from the mainline. Most of the time this is perfectly fine. Sometimes it's not. When Rites Go Wrong​So what happens when a Rite's Divergence drifts too far? When that eccentric archbishop in Cologne starts teaching things that make Rome deeply uncomfortable? The Head of Faith can declare a Divergent Rite a Heresy through a Papal Bull decision or an Ecumenical Council. The Rite Head then faces a choice: disband and return to the fold, or double down and become a new Faith entirely. If they choose the latter, congratulations - you now have a full-blown heretical offshoot Faith on your hands, complete with its own Head of Faith, its own teachings, and its own followers who are no longer part of the Church. Particularly powerful Rites can also turn Schismatic. These cannot be declared heretical outright and instead triggers a theological crisis within the faith, which must either be laboriously healed or permitted to lead to an actual schism, where the unified faith turns into two distinct ones. We'll cover the full mechanics of heresies and schisms in a dedicated dev diary. For now, the key takeaway is that these emerge organically from the interplay of characters, their Traits, Tenets, and Rites rather than being solely scripted events - which means your game's religious landscape will be genuinely unpredictable. View attachment 1480428 The College of Cardinals​Of course, none of this matters much if you can't influence who decides what is right and wrong - i.e., who sits on the Papal Throne. Cardinals are appointed by the Pope, and in the College of Cardinals electoral system their most important task is to vote for his successor. But it's not as simple as "highest score wins" - there are three candidate tracks, representing the most politically connected candidate, the most personally devout, and the most radical or reformist. Cardinals sort themselves by theological alignment, and unaligned Cardinals are the swing votes that determine the outcome. As a ruler, you will be able to work through specific Cardinals to influence the vote and sway support toward your preferred candidate. You can see who the projected candidates are and what Core teachings they would impose on all of Catholicism if elected - letting you assess, before the vote, how the next Pope might affect your character's Spiritual Fulfillment and the direction of the faith. Naturally, putting a member of your own dynasty on the papal throne is the ultimate prize. A friendly Pope can smooth over your sins, crush your enemies with excommunication, and redirect the wealth of the Church toward your ambitions. An unfriendly Pope could do all of those things to you. How Papal elections work also changes with the start date. In 867, only clergy in Rome and Italy may vote, with strong influence from the Italian political situation. By 1066, the College of Cardinals is fully established and much more susceptible to the machinations of ambitious rulers across Europe. We'll get into the full details of this system in a later diary. The Church on the Map​To make all of this work, we're adding Ecclesiastical Titles, a new geographic layer representing the archdiocese and patriarchate regions that form the administrative backbone of the Church. Each Ecclesiastical Title has a capital seat corresponding to a metropolitan city, and an archbishop (or patriarch, depending on your tradition) who holds it. These characters collect a share of all bishopric taxes within their region, giving them real economic power. The capital seat of an Ecclesiastical Title can be the site of a Cathedral. Cathedral construction is a Great Project that takes a long time and, in typical Great Project fashion, can be contributed to by rulers in the same region. Once complete, the cathedral is represented by a Domicile (and, of course, a model on the map!), which the holder of the Ecclesiastical Title can upgrade to serve a variety of functions. All this wealth poured into cathedral construction and development translates directly into determining the importance and ecclesiastical power of the metropolitan's seat. Raise a promising young theologian, and perhaps one day you will see him in charge of all the faithful at Reims. Ecclesiastical Title holders have more leeway than other characters in practicing and teaching Rites that diverge from the mainline, particularly if their importance starts to challenge that of Rome's. Due to this, archbishops have a greater tendency to be in charge of their own Rite or follow divergent Rites. This is something you can leverage as a ruler, if you find a way to hold sway over your archbishop. The Pope and other organized faith leaders will have a Church Treasury, separate from their personal wealth, which is filled by taxes and tithes from bishoprics across their faith. The Church Treasury can only be spent on Church business: funding temple construction, Great Projects, holy war funding and mercenaries, commissioning religious Artifacts, siphoned into the priest's pockets, and so on. This means the Catholic Church can be relatively wealthy or relatively poor depending on how large it is, how many heresies are siphoning off tax income, and how wisely (or corruptly) its various representatives spend what they collect. The Religious map mode has been updated to let you drill down from the faith level to the Rite level, so you can see at a glance how the religious landscape is fracturing (or consolidating) across the map. Combined with the new Ecclesiastical Titles, our aim is to make the Church feel like a living institution with geographic presence, internal politics, and real power. Revised Faith Creation and Conversion​With all these new systems for how faiths evolve organically, you might be wondering what happens to the old "stockpile Piety, click button, design custom faith from scratch" flow. The short answer is: it's been replaced by something we think will be much more interesting. Creating a new Faith now means working through the Rite system. You start by shaping the beliefs and practices within your current faith - elevating Tenets, nurturing a divergent Rite, building enough theological credibility and political power to either schism on your own terms or force the Head of Faith's hand. Your heresy has a causal history, it didn't spring fully formed from the mind of one eccentric king. Instead it grew, spread, and eventually became too big to ignore. We feel that's a much better story, and it also means the AI will produce more believable religious landscapes over the course of a campaign. Conversion has gotten a similar treatment. Through the new Study Faith scheme, you can learn the Doctrines and Tenets of another religious practice over time - whether from a foreign spouse, a court guest, or a neighboring realm. As you study, you can work newly learned ideas into your personal practice or even have them adopted officially through your Rite. At the conclusion of the scheme you also have the option to convert outright, with the transition being smoother (or rougher) depending on how much theological common ground actually exists between where you started and where you're going. You can still convert to any faith in the game, and we're not hard-locking any player fantasies behind this change. While this will undoubtedly make creating a new Faith more challenging, we think you'll find that faiths which evolve through play - through characters making choices, Rites drifting, and theological ideas spreading from person to person - feel considerably more alive than ones assembled from a menu. View attachment 1480427 Holy Order types​Holy Orders are also getting some love, with Monastic Orders distinguished from Military Orders. Monastic Orders lease temples rather than castles, and serve as centers of learning and theological development. Rulers can found them just like Military Orders, with some control over how they are shaped, though they become independent entities after that. Both Monastic and Military Orders can now expand across the map by establishing new abbeys, and will gain and lose power over the course of the game. All Holy Orders now have their own unique Rite, informed by the choices made during their founding. This means they can become centers of divergent practice - potential allies for reformist rulers, or targets for an orthodox Church cracking down on heterodoxy. Monastic Holy Orders serve as an alternate means of creating and nurturing divergent Rites and are meant as a counterforce to the Vatican's influence. Players can become Patrons of Holy Orders and can interact with Holy Order leaders in new ways, including sending family members to specific Orders and inviting Orders into their realm by offering them land. View attachment 1480430 Saints​What would Catholicism be without its Saints? (Okay, yeah, well, Protestantism, probably.) While the Saint Trait and Living Saint Legends already exist in the game, we're now adding several dozen historical Saints for you to adore, plus a path to become canonized after death for your pious deeds. What exactly this means we'll get further into in another dev diary, but for the moment let me tease that it has a lot to do with Spiritual Fulfillment, Personal Tenets, and Artifacts. Having a Saint in your family tree is, of course, very beneficial to you and your dynasty - especially when dealing with the Church. Play as the Pope​Yes, you will be able to play as the Pope. Theocratic characters with landed titles become playable, including the Pope. This means wielding the Church Treasury (funded by taxation across Christendom), appointing Cardinals, declaring heresies, calling Ecumenical Councils, excommunicating rulers who displease you, and generally being the most influential person in medieval Europe. It also means navigating the expectations of every Catholic ruler simultaneously. Good luck! It's worth noting that despite making this previously unplayable class of characters available, the expansion is emphatically not about playing as clergy trying to seize control of the Church. It is about establishing the Church as machinery which the player, typically in the role of a secular ruler, can strategically interact with and get pushback from. Our goal is to ensure that if you find yourself in the role of a landed character with real influence in church matters, you can still continue playing your dynasty - now with access to more levers to pull - rather than Game Over. Make the most of it! Puppets​As a secular ruler, you typically don't have a whole lot of say in Church matters. Sure, you can get your Court Chaplain to convert a county for you, or sponsor a Cathedral, or petition the Pope to lift an excommunication. But control over who teaches what, how Ecclesiastical Titles should be organized, whether an Ecumenical Council is called and what it should be about, or who should be the next Pope, these are matters only clergy have a say in. All of these new systems create a richly dynamic religious landscape, but as a secular ruler, you'll want to do more than watch from the sidelines while the expansion plays itself. It would be strange and ahistorical to give secular rulers direct ways of imposing change in most cases though, so what we're doing instead is giving you Puppets to toy with. The Puppet system is a new mechanic that lets you act through another character for specific actions in a specific context. Think of it as "your guy on the inside" who can get things done for you, assuming you have enough leverage on them. In the expansion this guy is your Court Chaplain. If you can get and maintain a Hook on them, there are certain things they can do on your behalf: for example, if they are a Rite Head they could start teaching a new Tenet, or even start a new Rite; construct a Cathedral with Church Treasury according to your specifications; and much more. If your Court Chaplain is also an Archbishop or Cardinal, they get access to more ways to reshape the Church and its practices. The Puppet system is not specific to Church matters but is also extensible to other domains that we will explore in future expansions, and will even be accessible to modders to create their own ways of manipulating other characters directly. Chalcedonian Christianity​As you might have intuited from a lot of the things I've discussed above, we are also addressing the biggest crime against the historical representation of the Christian Church currently in the game: the lack of a unified Christian church in 867, and the artificial distinction between Orthodoxy and Catholicism across start dates in lieu of an organic schism. In the 867 bookmark, most of Christianity (with notable exceptions of the Coptic and Apostolic Faiths) will be unified under one Faith called Chalcedonian Christianity, with multiple Rites such as the aforementioned Insular and Mozarabic Rites, Carolingian Christianity which directly threatens the Vatican's dominance, and the Patriarchates of Constantinople, Antioch, and Alexandria. It's unclear whether Christianity's most important seat is in Rome or Constantinople, so the Faith won't have a proper Head yet. Other Rites will emerge as the game goes on, and a Christian Church Situation will track the tensions both within the faith and with its neighbors. As the Situation moves into new phases due to the natural actions of the various central characters or the outcomes of Ecumenical Councils, the fracture between east and west may become untenable. It's a bit of a novel approach for CK3, but we hope this way of telling the centuries-long dynamic story of the medieval church will be fun and exciting to follow in your campaigns! What's Next​We have a lot of dev diaries ahead of us to explore each of these systems in detail, and more features, additions, and tweaks relating to Christianity and Faiths overall which I have not mentioned here. Keep in mind that everything you've read today is under active development, is subject to change, and your feedback - as always - will directly influence the final product. We cannot wait to hear what you think! In nomine Patris, et Filii, et Spiritus Sancti. Amen. Click to expand...

> Cyhort said: The only other religion that's as important to this time period in the main area of the game as Christianity is Islam. I know people hate hearing this and I'm going to get downvoted and people are going to bring out that dev quote about wishing they could change the name again, but the game is called Crusader Kings. Regardless of how the scope of the series has evolved over time, the core of it is still the struggle between Europe and the Middle East, Christianity and Islam. And since most of the player base is either in Europe or America, (and I'm pretty sure most players play in Europe) two sections of the world that were shaped by Christianity and where Christianity is still (for now) the dominant religion and the religion most customers will be familiar with, it makes perfect sense from both a business standpoint and a gameplay standpoint to start with a Christianity rework. What you're doing is you're lumping every non-Christian religion in the game into one faceless, identity-less mass and using that to act like Christianity is a small part of the game, when the exact opposite is true. It's a major part of the game, and a part that was majorly undercooked for years. Almost inexcusably so. But if that doesn't convince you, think of it this way. Would you rather the devs focus on a system that's meant to apply to every religion equally, most likely ending up with something bland and flavorless that doesn't accurately represent any religion? Or would you rather they focus on one religion at a time, putting as much effort into making it as dynamic and accurate as possible? For me, the latter will win out every time no matter what religion we're talking about. But YMMV. Click to expand...

The eastern churches recognized Rome as primus inter pares, first among equals, to them it was more a position of honor, not of authority. for rome to organize and perhaps lead a council is fine, but at no point Rome, for the eastern churches, can give orders to them. To put the bishop of Rome as the leader of the Church, is to frame the whole situation as the eastern church rebelling against the rightful leader of the Church, When in reality it was more the Bishop of Rome trying to assert it's dominance over the rest of the spiritual leaderships of a Church with a lot of issues. Which is a bit of a theme with the pope, trying the same thing with temporal leaders (donation of constantine, fight with the HRE etc...)

## Reply 330 — participant_240 · 2026-04-08 · post-31220311

Incredible development diary, thank you for the amazing work. That said, allow me to once again advocate for the appearance of military orders and, by extension, future religious orders. Their appearance does not have to be in chronological order; I think it makes much more sense for it to be based on the geographical location and culture of the founder. Example: A Christian Iberian kingdom is the first to found an order; it should not be the Templars, it has to be the Order of Santiago, the Order of Calatrava, etc. (depending on the culture and the geography where it is created). It is very anticlimactic to found an order that has nothing to do with your culture. If you are French or something similar, the Templars have to appear; if you are German (or some variant), the Teutons, and so on. Historically, these orders, apart from the religious aspect, had a cultural motive. From a gameplay perspective, I prefer the Templars to arise later rather than being created by a diferent culture. By the way, can military orders be excommunicated? Will it be possible to recreate or dynamically succeed the trial of the Templars? Thank you very much for the incredible work.

## Reply 353 — participant_004 · 2026-04-08 · post-31220866

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> lachek said: When Rites Go Wrong​So what happens when a Rite's Divergence drifts too far? When that eccentric archbishop in Cologne starts teaching things that make Rome deeply uncomfortable? The Head of Faith can declare a Divergent Rite a Heresy through a Papal Bull decision or an Ecumenical Council. The Rite Head then faces a choice: disband and return to the fold, or double down and become a new Faith entirely. If they choose the latter, congratulations - you now have a full-blown heretical offshoot Faith on your hands, complete with its own Head of Faith, its own teachings, and its own followers who are no longer part of the Church. Particularly powerful Rites can also turn Schismatic. These cannot be declared heretical outright and instead triggers a theological crisis within the faith, which must either be laboriously healed or permitted to lead to an actual schism, where the unified faith turns into two distinct ones. We'll cover the full mechanics of heresies and schisms in a dedicated dev diary. For now, the key takeaway is that these emerge organically from the interplay of characters, their Traits, Tenets, and Rites rather than being solely scripted events - which means your game's religious landscape will be genuinely unpredictable. View attachment 1480428 ​ Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->

## Reply 359 — participant_148 · 2026-04-08 · post-31220985

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Ladislaus Vessys said: 2) Could you, for all that is holy (pun intended), allow us -or the characters who lead a rite- to pick (local) holy sites specific to that rite, in addition to the universal ones? Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
This would be great. To be honest, I'd love to see an extension of this further, so that a rite's local holy sites can grow to become new holy sites or supplant old ones as the rite's Ecclesiastical Title becomes more influential and the associated realms become larger, wealthier, and more powerful. For example, Santiago is currently a Catholic holy site, and in the present day the Camino de Santiago is an extremely prominent and popular pilgrimage route, but in 867 it was only just beginning to be elevated to prominence following the discovery of relics of St James there. Large numbers of pilgrims from outside Spain didn't take the Camino until the 11th century. It would be cool if there were a system to model this kind of shift over the course of a campaign. Similarly, it would be cool if new practices could be incorporated from rite to rite or pilgrim sites as the faithful come into contact with each other. For example, the Via Dolorosa/Stations of the Cross was a practice that pilgrims would perform in Jerusalem that proliferated as pilgrims came back home and took it with them, leading to the Stations being represented on church walls or in gardens, becoming a very well-known practice. The Stations could be specified to work this way manually in the code (maybe some kinds of fasting could be another example), but it would be cool to see pilgrimages having an effect on tenet spread by "exporting" local observances/tenets around the religion's territory, leading to the overall evolution of the faith.

To the point of view of the western churches, yes, it was a rebellion. But for the east, it was the bishop of Rome trying to be more than what he was. At the start date of 867, the pope was not just the leader of most of the western churches, but also a temporal leader, the bishops of rome clearly were using the absence of imperial authority in the west to impose himself, not just as a spiritual leader, but later on as the temporal leader of the christian world. For the eastern part, in their point of view, it was a pope trying to dominate the others, and to them he crossed some boundaries. And the emperor in the east did not dissapear, and was clearly using the his authority to influence the church, the coptic church finally seperated due to them not liking the emperor involving himself in the affair of the Church, and the final blow was the council of chalcedon.... My point is that making ingame the pope as the leader of the Chalcedonian church, when famously the roman emperor was still around and influencing things, is taking the side of the catholic church. And while there is plenty of proofs of the domination of Rome other the others, there is also proofs of eastern Churches resisting the attempt of Rome to dominate others, and very specifically Constantinople, where the presence of a strong imperial authority would not allow the independence that the Bishop of Rome took for himself.

## Reply 366 — participant_257 · 2026-04-08 · post-31221320

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> lachek said: Chalcedonian Christianity​As you might have intuited from a lot of the things I've discussed above, we are also addressing the biggest crime against the historical representation of the Christian Church currently in the game: the lack of a unified Christian church in 867, and the artificial distinction between Orthodoxy and Catholicism across start dates in lieu of an organic schism. In the 867 bookmark, most of Christianity (with notable exceptions of the Coptic and Apostolic Faiths) will be unified under one Faith called Chalcedonian Christianity, with multiple Rites such as the aforementioned Insular and Mozarabic Rites, Carolingian Christianity which directly threatens the Vatican's dominance, and the Patriarchates of Constantinople, Antioch, and Alexandria. It's unclear whether Christianity's most important seat is in Rome or Constantinople, so the Faith won't have a proper Head yet. Other Rites will emerge as the game goes on, and a Christian Church Situation will track the tensions both within the faith and with its neighbors. As the Situation moves into new phases due to the natural actions of the various central characters or the outcomes of Ecumenical Councils, the fracture between east and west may become untenable. It's a bit of a novel approach for CK3, but we hope this way of telling the centuries-long dynamic story of the medieval church will be fun and exciting to follow in your campaigns! Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
This part has both my favorite part of this and my least-favorite. Uniting all of Chalcedonian Christianity makes complete sense and is something players have been asking for since the CK2 start date got pushed back to a pre-1066 date. Awesome. However, theologically, it absolutely would be correct for Chalcedonian Christianity to have a single head in the Bishop of Rome. There should certainly be some variance in what that *actually* means for the different rites in-game, but the churches that became the Eastern Orthodox agreed that the Bishop of Rome was, at least, First Among Equals. Which means, de jure, that the Bishop of Rome is the head of the faith, in gameplay terms. It would seem that the best representation would be that the Greek Rites, in Chalcedonian Christianity, are very autonomous and tendentious in their relationship to Rome, but still respect some nominal authority of it. Anyway, I'm hoping that the game will be representing the ability to create Eastern Catholic Rites (that seems to be almost a necessary consequence of this direction of the game), and I'm also excited for ecclesiastical titles.

So maybe this little bit of love for holy orders now would help with setting it up more later?

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Rites spread along Ecclesiastical Title borders - a new geographic layer representing the ancient archdiocese regions - and characters within a Rite's domain can choose to adopt it. Over time, neighboring archdioceses may adopt the same Rite, creating regional blocs of distinct religious practice within Catholicism. This naturally soft-contains Rites within realm borders while still permitting the kind of organic drift and cross-pollination that made medieval Christianity so fascinatingly messy. Looks fantastic! I'm filled with hope, and also with dread that things like, "if you manage to get leverage on your chaplain" turns into "push the "find secret button" ruin the actual experience. A few thoughts: Please tell me that there are ways for these “Ecclesiastical Title” borders to change. For example, as you Christianize the pagans of northern Europe, those titles should probably start of as large areas (like a frontier conversion region) but then, as it becomes a more core part of the Christian region, bishops (or other important clerical figures) in the area can take a vote to segment it into smaller diocese – probably creating more bishoprics in the process. Please tell me that it isn't ALL clear to you exactly who they'll be and what they support. ## Reply 371 — participant_260 · 2026-04-09 · post-31221549

Insular mentioned! Vindication? Might we get Scotland's culture reworked too? Anyway, this seems very, very interesting and on point. Adding an ecclesiastical titles layer to the map sounds brilliant, very "ambitious" to use a catchphrase, and more in line with how things worked than everyone being Henry VIII in waiting. Since church affairs are the focus, maybe there could be a system for non-ruler saints, since most saints weren't rulers. Not to mention, one of the defining thing about saints is that they're declared after they have died - during their lifetimes people have more mundane or varied opinions about them, and sometimes people highly respected going off the rails or into obscurity. You should have turbulent priests, not random high learning guys from 200 years ago briefly and bewilderingly returning in pop-up events. Perhaps a "Holy Person" system where characters can spawn with, or obtain the status of "Holy Man/Woman", maybe a bit like the "Conqueror" trait except for religion, which gives them their own agenda and makes them more activist in interacting with the new religious mechanics, such as founding or revitalizing Holy Orders, or pushing Rites (if that's the right term) towards certain directions. It would make more sense if they are more likely to spawn and result in sainthood if they are in line with the religion's existing "Rites", and risk becoming Heresiarchs if they are excessively divergent and/or influential. They (and rulers...) can have a "sainthood meter" which ticks up possibly during but certainly after their life, based on factors like their order (if applicable), culture, what rites are in fashion, and what side of schisms/disputes they were on. Not to mention their birthplace, associated locations, being martyred, and where their relics (which would be its own mechanic) are kept. Rulers and ecclesiastics could invest resources (money, prestige, piety) in making the case for their cause. Then the sainthood meter can trigger by itself or be approved by the religious leader(s) after it hits the threshold. (This depends on the time period and religious organizations). This Holy Person system could work for non-Christian religions whether or not they have saints or comparable concepts, and possibly tie into religious rebellions, great holy wars, learning about other religions and mysticism. You could also have a system where someone who kills or mistreats Holy People gets a special trait or modifier as "Persecutor of X", which is generally seen as bad if it's Holy People of your own religion, but could be "good" if it's Holy People of other religions or heresies. Thomas Becket perhaps the most famous example. The reverse as "Patron" or "Protector" could be relevant too. Without more detail, I would hope the system for diverging beliefs and practices doesn't get too wacky too easily. It's a bit confusing if some terms like "rite" and "liturgy" have narrow meanings in reality but seem like they might have specific applications within CK3 referring to gameplay concepts for regional church organizations? If I'm understanding the description, a religion is like a pool of playing/trading cards which represent acceptable beliefs and practices; the Pope/religious leader draws a "hand", the player character's personal beliefs and their local church's practice are more "hands", and if they match cards with the Pope's hand, they get bonuses? While if the local church or the player character's own beliefs draw "cards" which are not part of the religion's pool (eg: human sacrifices to the sun god), then the Pope/nearest religious authority begins to monitor the situation with deep concern? I would hope that there's distinctions between "practices" and "beliefs"; perhaps "liturgy" is its own category or spread between the two. It's not clear to me if the "Archbishops" (which as people have pointed out should probably be called something else like Primate or Metropolitan) directly change the Rites themselves like secular rulers do in the current "I'm changing our religion" system. Synods in the local/regional context and Councils in the overall context would be more likely to be needed to make big changes stick. They could allow other things to be done/decrees issued with relevance for secular conflicts which overlap with ecclesiastical conflicts. The dynamic in Stellaris where the galactic council needs to convene to get anything done by small degrees but goes away for 10 years at a time seems strangely desirable. To extend the "deck of cards" analogy, the "card pool" representing the range of theological opinions could shrink as complex beliefs are codified, and some cards which were implicitly tolerable no longer are accepted, or get put on the banlist. "Development of Doctrine", I'm blanking on any other terms for the concept. This is probably a functional way to distinguish heated disputes from heresies, which should tend to "break" churches instead of "bend" them, and prevent flip-flopping if every belief can be swapped in or out forever.

There should certainly be some variance in what that *actually* means for the different rites in-game, but the churches that became the Eastern Orthodox agreed that the Bishop of Rome was, at least, First Among Equals. Which means, de jure, that the Bishop of Rome is the head of the faith, in gameplay terms. Perhaps an acceptable compromise would be that the Pope is the head of faith, but there's some sort of modifier which limits the use of the normal head of faith powers and interactions - which could either be read as the power struggle between East and West limiting his practical influence (a Catholic view of history), or as authority being decentralised and the Pope being merely Primus Inter Pares (an Orthodox view). This could then be resolved through the mechanics introduced here, with the Pope asserting his authority and unlocking more head of faith abilities, while the East is either brought to heel or splits off, or in the other direction the East successfully imposing the decentralised Pentarchy permanently.

## Reply 374 — participant_257 · 2026-04-09 · post-31221663

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> ArchangelGabriel said: Perhaps an acceptable compromise would be that the Pope is the head of faith, but there's some sort of modifier which limits the use of the normal head of faith powers and interactions - which could either be read as the power struggle between East and West limiting his practical influence (a Catholic view of history), or as authority being decentralised and the Pope being merely Primus Inter Pares (an Orthodox view). Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I wouldn’t even call that a compromise, I think it is the most natural way to represent the 9th century Church. I think we can all agree that the Schism in 1054 was largely about the Papacy attempting to assert its supremacy (the disagreement, of course, is whether the Bishops of Rome were right to do so). So, in a pre-1054 framework, the extent of Rome’s authority is still debated, and it would be reasonable to represent that in a few different ways: - Have a “head of faith authority” mechanic, similar to various other mechanics in the game. Basically an equivalent of the crown authority law. Just as an idea, the tiers could be “Papal collegiality/primacy/supremacy.” “Primacy” could be the starting condition, while the Eastern church could be pushing for “collegiality” and the Western church could be pushing for “supremacy.” - Have different rites respect the authority of the Pope to different degrees. Probably a mix of both.

## Reply 391 — participant_257 · 2026-04-09 · post-31222488

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> DominusNovus said: I wouldn’t even call that a compromise, I think it is the most natural way to represent the 9th century Church. I think we can all agree that the Schism in 1054 was largely about the Papacy attempting to assert its supremacy (the disagreement, of course, is whether the Bishops of Rome were right to do so). So, in a pre-1054 framework, the extent of Rome’s authority is still debated, and it would be reasonable to represent that in a few different ways: - Have a “head of faith authority” mechanic, similar to various other mechanics in the game. Basically an equivalent of the crown authority law. Just as an idea, the tiers could be “Papal collegiality/primacy/supremacy.” “Primacy” could be the starting condition, while the Eastern church could be pushing for “collegiality” and the Western church could be pushing for “supremacy.” - Have different rites respect the authority of the Pope to different degrees. Probably a mix of both. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I've been mulling this over, and I think a good solution would be to have the 'Papal Authority' mechanic I list above, and each Rite will have its own preferred value for the Authority to be set to. For the various Western Rites (Latin, Insular, Mozarabic, any others), they should prefer 'Papal Supremacy.'. For the various Eastern Rites (Greek, Syriac, Coptic, etc) should prefer either 'Papal Primacy' or 'Papal Collegiality.' The issue should be an ongoing point of contention, and the matter can be resolved through Ecumenical Councils - mechanically, maybe you need successive councils all affirming the same authority, without variance on the issue. Its not a perfect canon law example, but it could be a matter of: one Council makes it a 'Discipline,' two Councils makes it a 'Doctrine,' and three Councils makes it a 'Dogma,' no longer open to debate. If Chalcedonian Christianity stays unified, its going to be difficult to get the matter resolved to everyone's satisfaction, and it should be right up there with Restoring the Roman Empire as a game goal. You can try to get each Rite to formally change it stance on Authority, which means they'll not only go along with your preferred stance in Councils, but they won't grumble about it afterward (this would be the 'better' solution). Or you can just browbeat them into accepting it in councils (hooks, military might, etc), and keep Christianity unified more forcefully, even if it means the other rites don't really like the solution, and will break away the moment they can. If the Church splits, then it becomes trivially easy for each side to enforce their view of things. And, from that point, it could be a matter of just expanding your Church's influence the more blunt way, and establishing new Rites for the locals (similar to the historical Eastern Catholic Rites, established in the early modern era).

## Reply 436 — participant_278 · 2026-04-10 · post-31224420

I believe the East Asia update from last time can be further improved by applying the logic of the new "Rite" system to secular governance. Just as "Rites" represent regional variations in faith, it would be great if secular lords could issue their own "Secular Edicts" for their specific domains. For example, in pre-modern Japan, Shudo (same-sex relations) was not a religious sin. However, some local lords (Daimyo) strictly prohibited it because they wanted to ensure that loyalty to the lord was prioritized over romantic relationships, and to prevent public disorder caused by violent conflicts (stabbings) over lovers. Instead of changing the entire faith's doctrine, a lord should be able to directly define certain acts as "Crimes" within their own territory. This kind of regional secular rule-making would make the gameplay for East Asia and other regions with strong secular authority much more authentic. Spoiler: Historical Context: Prohibitions for Order and Loyalty Following the traditions of monks, court nobles, and samurai, male-male eroticism spread to the commoners, establishing it as a form of commercial sex. In pre-modern Japan, these acts were not viewed as perversions or considered ethically problematic compared to heterosexual relations.However, even in the early Edo period, some domains began to strictly crack down on "shudo" (the way of the youth) among vassals. Notably, Ikeda Mitsumasa (1609-1682), the lord of the Himeji Domain, strictly forbade shudo within his house and banished vassals who violated the rule. Furthermore, in 1652, Wakashu Kabuki, which was a hotbed for boy prostitution, was banned by the Edo town magistrate's office.Mid-to-Late Edo Period: A Transition to a Less Prominent Era for Male-Male CultureBy the middle of the Edo period, male-male relationships began to be seen as a disruption of public morals because men were prioritizing their lovers over loyalty to their lords, and violent incidents over beautiful youths became frequent. Ikeda Mitsumasa, the lord of Himeji, strictly prohibited male-male acts and banished violators from his territory. Uesugi Harunori of the Yonezawa Domain ordered a strict crackdown on shudo in 1775, and the Edo Shogunate also carried out thorough moral policing during the Kyoho, Kansei, and Tenpo Reforms. In 1842, "kagemajaya" (male brothels) were banned. By the end of the Edo period, these practices gradually ceased to be conducted openly. 日本における同性愛 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org

## Reply 437 — participant_021 · 2026-04-10 · post-31224470

It would be an interesting character interaction, which would give a further reason to get the pope on side, if popes were able to ‘bless’ one side in a conflict (improving morale of the soldiers of that side?) or even grant a casus belli. I’ve got two specific historical examples in mind. The first is the Norman conquest of England, where Pope Alexander II gave his blessing to William, Duke of Normandy, for the invasion and provided a papal banner (could be an artefact in CK3) to signify approval. The second is the Anglo-Norman invasion of Ireland in 1155, where Pope Adrian IV issued a papal bull (could grant a casus belli in CK3) granting Henry II permission to enter Ireland to bring the Irish to a better form of the Christian faith and to bring them into obedience to the Roman Church.

definition by Gemini because I'm lazy: "the strategic process of creating a unique name, design, symbol, or reputation for a product or company to differentiate it from competitors and build long-term value" Now I guess you want this to be a "gotcha moment", but it does illustrate the link I gave. At first the branding of the game made sense with CK1, but with the direction taken with CK2, the serie is no longer mainly about crusader states and crusade in general, but more broadly about the medieval period, from which crusades and crusader kings are elements of this time period (important yes, but not the most important, once you go away from an euro-centric view of the time period). Since CK2 is one of the reason of the exploding popularity of PDX, the brand is just too strong to just rename it, therefore why it is a regret of the main designer of CK.

## Reply 456 — participant_284 · 2026-04-12 · post-31226782

Hello. Three ideas below that I believe complement what the dev diary describes — each one connects to the announced mechanics rather than asking for something entirely new. Saint cults as a bridge between Spiritual Fulfillment and Rite Divergence: The diary describes two systems that feel like they're begging to be connected: Spiritual Fulfillment on the personal level, and Rite Divergence on the regional level. Here's what I'd propose as the link between them. When a ruler dies with very high Spiritual Fulfillment — having lived in genuine accordance with their faith — they should have a chance to spark a Local Cult in their home county or archdiocese. Not full canonization (that's the Pope's domain and already seems covered), but the informal grassroots veneration that actually drove medieval religious identity. Think of how the cult of Thomas Becket in Canterbury, or Martin of Tours in the Loire valley, shaped local devotion independently of Rome. Mechanically, an active Local Cult could act as an anchor that either stabilises a Rite in that region (preventing drift) or accelerates divergence if the saint's veneration runs contrary to Rome's current doctrinal direction. A dynasty that produces multiple saints in a single region would organically build a distinct religious identity — which could eventually feed into heresy or a formal Rite split, without anyone deliberately choosing to break away. This makes Spiritual Fulfillment feel consequential beyond the ruler's own lifetime, gives secular players a long-term religious legacy goal beyond "put my dynasty on the papal throne," and keeps Rite Divergence feeling emergent rather than mechanical. The Inquisition as a pastoral tool, not just a punishment mechanic: Without an Inquisition system, the Pope feels like a passive observer to heresy rather than an active shepherd. The diary talks beautifully about Rite Divergence — faiths drifting organically, heresies emerging from the interplay of characters and tenets. That's exactly right. But drift needs a counter-force, and historically that counter-force wasn't armies. It was inquisitors. My suggestion: the Inquisition as the Pope's primary tool for spiritual reconquest — distinct from crusades, which are military. Playing as the Pope should mean sending Inquisitor characters (drawn from the clergy pool, with their own traits and skills) into archdioceses that are drifting. Their job isn't just to burn heretics — it's to investigate, persuade, pressure local bishops, and report back. Success slows Rite Drift and improves a region's Spiritual Fulfillment toward orthodoxy. Failure — or a brutal Inquisitor — radicalises the population and accelerates the very schism you were trying to prevent. This creates a genuinely different feel for Papal play. A secular ruler wins by taking land. The Pope wins by winning hearts. The sword of the Church shouldn't be a sword at all — it should be a question, a trial, a choice offered to a wavering bishop at midnight. Mechanically, this ties Rite Divergence directly to a responsive papal action, gives the College of Cardinals something to meaningfully debate (how harsh should the Inquisition be?), and ensures that even a non-military Pope has a significant way to shape the religious map. Missionaries as the offensive counterpart to the Inquisition: If the Inquisition is about holding the faithful, missionaries are about expanding the flock — and right now the game treats conversion as a passive side-effect of conquest rather than a deliberate act of the Church. The current Court Chaplain "convert province" action is a placeholder, not a system. What I'd want to see instead: the Pope (or any bishop) dispatching missionary characters into pagan territories as a deliberate, multi-stage campaign. First establishing a foothold — a small chapel, a sympathetic local lord — then preaching, then dealing with the backlash from pagan priests and resistant rulers. The tension should be real: a missionary who is too aggressive gets martyred and sets the cause back by decades, but one who is too accommodating starts syncretising local rites into the faith — which feeds directly back into the Rite Divergence system. The Christianity that reaches Scandinavia or Lithuania through missionaries might not look exactly like the Christianity in Rome, and that should be a feature, not a bug. For secular rulers, this also opens a diplomatic angle: quietly funding a mission into your neighbour's pagan lands before invading gives you a legitimate casus belli and a population that's easier to integrate. Faith as statecraft, not just piety. Together, the Inquisition and the missionary system give the Church two directions to face: inward to correct drift, outward to expand. That's what the medieval Church actually did — and it would make Papal play feel genuinely distinct from anything else in the game.

## Reply 457 — participant_284 · 2026-04-12 · post-31226796

The dev diary reads genuinely well — the design intent is clear, the systems are interconnected, and the ambition is real. I've been following this space, so what follows isn't scepticism for its own sake — it's a set of specific implementation risks that I think are worth naming early, while the systems are still in active development. Thought 1 — Spiritual Fulfillment Comparing it to Stress sets a difficult precedent The diary explicitly frames Spiritual Fulfillment as "the spiritual counterpart to Stress." That framing is useful for communication, but Stress is one of the most consistently criticised systems in CK3 — largely because it resolves too easily and doesn't create sustained tension. If Spiritual Fulfillment follows the same gain/drain loop, it risks becoming a second counter that players optimise around rather than roleplay through. The deeper question is whether it feeds back into systems outside the character screen. If a ruler with very low Spiritual Fulfillment doesn't visibly affect their court's opinion, their clergy's loyalty, or their dynasty's reputation — it will feel decorative rather than structural. Thought 2 — Rite Divergence Organic emergence risks either never firing or always firing Making the Great Schism emergent rather than scripted is the right instinct historically — but Paradox has a documented track record of dynamic systems that collapse into one of two failure states: the event fires every game within a narrow window, or it never fires at all depending on AI behaviour. Neither outcome serves the design goal. The second risk is cosmetic divergence — many small Rite variants that are mechanically indistinguishable from each other. Diversity without difference is an illusion of depth. If a drifted Rite doesn't produce meaningfully different tenets, different relationships with Rome, or different gameplay pressures, players will stop noticing it after the first campaign. Thought 3 — Playable Pope The Puppet mechanic could undermine the value of playing as Pope This is the most structurally delicate tension in the whole expansion. If a secular ruler can exert strong influence over the Papacy through their Court Chaplain's Puppet mechanic, the question becomes: why play as the Pope directly? The two experiences need to feel genuinely asymmetric — not just the same power expressed through a different character. The Pope also has no dynastic succession by blood, which is one of CK3's core gameplay loops. If that fundamental difference isn't addressed with equally fundamental mechanical replacements — a legacy measured in doctrine, in saints, in the health of the Church rather than in heirs — Papal play risks feeling like a regular ruler with extra steps and fewer options. Thought 4 — College of Cardinals Three candidate tracks can quickly become a solved meta The three-track electoral system — political, devout, reformist — is elegant on paper. The risk is that one track proves consistently stronger than the others within a few months of release, at which point the choice stops being a choice. For this to have lasting strategic depth, each track needs to carry genuine costs and genuine benefits that shift depending on the current religious situation — not just different flavour text leading to similar outcomes. Cardinals also need to be characters first and vote-blocs second. If they're primarily stat blocks without personal relationships, ambitions, and pressure points, the political drama of a Papal election collapses into a numbers game. Thought 5 — Ecclesiastical Titles A new geographic layer risks interface overload CK3's UI is already under strain from the number of overlapping systems. Adding a full archdiocese layer — with archbishops holding real economic power through bishopric taxes — is the right historical move, but it needs to be surfaced carefully. If players can't immediately understand how an Ecclesiastical Title relates to their existing feudal structure, it will either be ignored or produce confusion rather than engagement. Archbishops with genuine economic leverage also risk falling into the binary that has affected similar powerful vassals in CK3: either trivially manageable or a source of frustrating attrition with no interesting middle ground. Thought 6 — Church Situation in 867 Situations have historically underperformed their design intent The Christian Church Situation is being used to tell "the centuries-long dynamic story of the medieval church," which is an ambitious goal for a system that players have consistently noted fades into the background after the early game. For this to work, the Situation needs to generate active decision points — not just passive progress bars — and the Ecumenical Councils mentioned need to produce irreversible consequences that genuinely alter the religious map. A Council that can always be undone in the next generation removes all dramatic weight. Thought 7 — Scope Orthodox and Eastern Christian players may feel left behind The diary is refreshingly honest that this expansion focuses on Catholicism, with other branches handled secondarily. That's a reasonable scope decision. The concern is that previous DLC have created a visible gap between richly updated factions and those left on older systems — and that gap generates lasting frustration. If Orthodox gameplay in particular remains structurally unchanged while Catholic gameplay is transformed, playing in the eastern half of the map will feel like a different and lesser game. Even a clear public roadmap for when Eastern Christianity will receive comparable attention would go a long way toward managing that expectation.

## Reply 458 — participant_284 · 2026-04-12 · post-31226823

Before diving into suggestions for the new expansion, I think it's worth naming the existing problems clearly — because the risk isn't just that the new systems fail on their own terms, but that they get layered on top of foundations that have been broken for years. Problem 1 — Piety as a resource Piety has no tension — it's a number that only ever needs to go up The core design flaw is that piety functions as a pure accumulation resource with a single correct direction. Every event with a religious choice has an objectively right answer — the one that gives you piety — and players know it. One of the developers even noted, somewhat ironically, that players skip event text entirely and go straight to the tooltip to find the piety-positive option. That's not engagement with faith. That's resource optimisation wearing a cassock. The deeper absurdity: a character who is excommunicated, commits incest, murders their own Pope, and practises witchcraft still passively accumulates piety every month if they happen to have the right personality traits. The number has completely decoupled from what it's supposed to represent. Problem 2 — The Pope The Pope functions as a piggy bank, not a spiritual authority In practice, the Pope is the wealthiest character on the map — he collects taxes from every Catholic temple holding in the game. The primary interaction most players have with him is "Ask for Gold" every decade for 250 piety. He is a financial instrument with excommunication as an afterthought. His spiritual authority over Catholic rulers is nearly absent in actual gameplay terms, which makes the historical weight of the papal relationship completely invisible. Excommunication — which brought Holy Roman Emperors to their knees in real history — is currently either ignored through Dread, trivially reversed, or bypassed entirely. It carries no sustained existential pressure. Problem 3 — Faith is a passive status You belong to a faith, but you never actually practise it As the diary itself acknowledges: if you start as a Catholic, you have some doctrines and tenets, and that's essentially it until you convert or found a new faith. There's no act of worship, no liturgical calendar, no moment where being Catholic asks something of you personally beyond trait modifiers. Religion evokes flavour but demands nothing. For a game set in a world where the Church structured every aspect of life from birth to death, this is a significant gap. The clergy are similarly inert. Bishops exist as tax nodes and council slots. They don't preach, don't pressure, don't organise — they administer. Problem 4 — Heresy and fervor Heresy spawns from the top down, not the bottom up Every major historical heresy — Catharism, Waldensianism, Hussitism — was a popular movement that built pressure from below before it ever involved rulers. In CK3, the entire heresy system is ruler-driven: you spend piety to found a new faith, and heresies appear as mechanical consequences of low fervor rather than as genuine social movements. Common people, monks, and wandering preachers play no role whatsoever. Fervor itself degrades almost automatically through background events involving immoral bishops, creating a constant background noise of heretical faiths that feel random rather than historically rooted. There's rarely a sense that a heresy emerged from something real. Problem 5 — Piety in the late game Piety becomes meaningless once the major religious goals are achieved Once crusades are won, a new faith is founded, and the Pope is either an ally or a vassal, there is nothing meaningful left to spend piety on. It accumulates indefinitely with no interesting sink. This is a structural problem: piety should be a living resource that reflects ongoing engagement with faith, not a score that peaks and then stagnates. A Catholic emperor in 1200 who has done everything the game asks of him religiously has no further religious life. The reason I'm raising these here is not to be pessimistic about the DLC — the design direction in the diary is genuinely exciting. But if Spiritual Fulfillment, the Inquisition, and Rite Divergence are built on the same foundations that produced these problems, they will inherit them. The new systems need to replace these patterns, not sit alongside them. Oh my goodness, I think It’s as if I’d written my diary; I hope you’ll read this

<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I did think on this a bit when readying the dev diary: Organic emergence: it would be a shame if the situation for the Great Schism just tends to amble along without player intervention. Without the Schism, a lot of the drama of the medieval church would be missing - no Splintered Crusade (I imagine it will be locked behind the Schism happening once the DLC is active), no efforts to bring back church unity. I think I'd be less upset if it were on the overtuned side and almost always happened without player intervention though. Cosmetic divergence: aside from the mainstream Latin West and Greek East, I do think Rites could feel like they have very small differences. Like, if they just have different tenet focuses, then I suppose they'll make life a bit easier or harder for characters with certain personality traits. But since characters come and go and Rites are more long-term formations, I don't think we'll be trying to promote or undermine a Rite just because it makes our current character more easily feel spiritually fulfilled. Perhaps some of the bigger rules changes that I can think of might make a difference - you may want to have access to concubines while still being a good Catholic, and if you protect the Insular RIte that could make it possible. Maybe other Rites could also allow for raiding, or for early primogeniture? Rites with those features might attract player interest, and they may also be interesting in that it would take more work to keep them from being declared heresies.

## Reply 464 — participant_284 · 2026-04-12 · post-31227270

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Videogames said: I did think on this a bit when readying the dev diary: Organic emergence: it would be a shame if the situation for the Great Schism just tends to amble along without player intervention. Without the Schism, a lot of the drama of the medieval church would be missing - no Splintered Crusade (I imagine it will be locked behind the Schism happening once the DLC is active), no efforts to bring back church unity. I think I'd be less upset if it were on the overtuned side and almost always happened without player intervention though. Cosmetic divergence: aside from the mainstream Latin West and Greek East, I do think Rites could feel like they have very small differences. Like, if they just have different tenet focuses, then I suppose they'll make life a bit easier or harder for characters with certain personality traits. But since characters come and go and Rites are more long-term formations, I don't think we'll be trying to promote or undermine a Rite just because it makes our current character more easily feel spiritually fulfilled. Perhaps some of the bigger rules changes that I can think of might make a difference - you may want to have access to concubines while still being a good Catholic, and if you protect the Insular RIte that could make it possible. Maybe other Rites could also allow for raiding, or for early primogeniture? Rites with those features might attract player interest, and they may also be interesting in that it would take more work to keep them from being declared heresies. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Overtuning solves one problem by creating another. Your instinct about the Schism is understandable, but I'd push back a little on "better overtuned than undertuned." If it reliably fires in every campaign within a predictable window, it functionally becomes a scripted event with better packaging — the organic emergence is cosmetic rather than real. The failure mode isn't just "never happens," it's equally "always happens the same way." The actual goal, I think, is that it happens differently each time: early in one campaign because a player actively destabilised Eastern relations, late in another because someone worked hard to hold unity, and occasionally not at all. That's a much harder design problem than tuning a slider — it requires the Schism to be genuinely sensitive to player and AI behaviour rather than running on a background timer. Whether Paradox can pull that off is exactly the right question to be asking before release. Mechanical bonuses attract optimisers, not believers. The examples you raise — concubines, raiding, early primogeniture — would definitely give players a reason to care about Rites. But I think they'd attract the wrong kind of caring. If the Insular Rite allows concubinage, players will gravitate toward it not because they're roleplaying an Irish monk, but because it's a stronger build. That's the same problem that already exists with Tenets — people pick Warrior Monks for the combat bonus, not for religious conviction. Rites risk becoming another optimisation layer rather than a source of genuine religious identity. The distinction I'd draw is this: mechanical differences that create conflict of interest are more interesting than mechanical differences that create stat advantages. A Rite that permits married clergy isn't compelling because it buffs something — it's compelling because Rome will want to suppress it, local lords will want to protect it, and bishops caught between the two will have to pick a side. That tension is what makes a Rite feel like a living religious tradition rather than a perk tree. Your point about Rites needing more work to avoid being declared heresies is actually the strongest part of the argument — that friction is exactly where the interesting gameplay lives. A Rite that's one controversial Council away from becoming a full schism is far more engaging than one that simply unlocks a doctrine.

## Reply 470 — participant_074 · 2026-04-13 · post-31228721

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> VadimKodeks said: Overtuning solves one problem by creating another. Your instinct about the Schism is understandable, but I'd push back a little on "better overtuned than undertuned." Whether Paradox can pull that off is exactly the right question to be asking before release. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> VadimKodeks said: Mechanical bonuses attract optimisers, not believers. The examples you raise — concubines, raiding, early primogeniture — would definitely give players a reason to care about Rites. But I think they'd attract the wrong kind of caring. If the Insular Rite allows concubinage, players will gravitate toward it not because they're roleplaying an Irish monk, but because it's a stronger build. That's the same problem that already exists with Tenets — people pick Warrior Monks for the combat bonus, not for religious conviction. Rites risk becoming another optimisation layer rather than a source of genuine religious identity. The distinction I'd draw is this: mechanical differences that create conflict of interest are more interesting than mechanical differences that create stat advantages. A Rite that permits married clergy isn't compelling because it buffs something — it's compelling because Rome will want to suppress it, local lords will want to protect it, and bishops caught between the two will have to pick a side. <!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I agree for sure that a dynamic, narrative-driven encounter with the Schism would be best. But, I'd still prefer a scripted event over a dynamic system that fails to fire most of the time because it's undertuned. If the drama and narrative of the Great Schism, the Fourth Crusade, and subsequent attempts at reconciliation are often just not unlocked because things basically just go ok in Christendom throughout the game, then that's a system that's less fun than if the kickoff to the Schism were overtuned or just hard-scripted. A decently overtuned system can also make for a good challenge keeping the Schism from happening, and making it a challenge is one way of making what would otherwise be a static task into something fun. The quality of design is a big factor though. For example, when AuH came out I did a Japan run where my goal was to keep the Ritsuryo state intact and prevent a slide into Soryo warrior rule. The game is tuned such that, without human intervention, Japan will follow the historical route and slide into warrior rule. Standing athwart history and keeping civil rule and the Fujiwara regency sounds like it could make for a good narrative for a run, right? Well, in practice the factors that push Japan to warrior rule are difficult for the Ritsuryo AI to handle, but for a human player they're not difficult to overcome but they are annoying. You just slap down every governor who declares a Soryo fief. The rate at which governors do that increases over the timeline, and it appears not to be affected by factors such as the regent's legitimacy or dread. There's also no capstone mechanic to make it so that you've "won" vs. the Soryo problem and can move on to other concerns. It's just Soryo slap-down after slap-down all the way to 1453. (Interestingly if you play as the emperor, there is a capstone Restore Imperial Rule decision that gets rid of the ruling regency for good, but doesn't settle the Soryo issue. And then on the other hand, if you're playing on the Soryo track the Declare Shogunate decision kills off the Ritsuryo state for good - go figure.) All that to say, I hope that this design makes it so that if you want to preserve Chalcedonian Christianity and not have the Schism in your run, it should be both challenging and interesting to pull off. If you're not working to preserve the unity, the system should be dynamic and interesting but probably weighted towards the Schism happening because there's interesting content behind the Schism. Eh, most players aren't going to be True Believers about doctrinal issues in the different Rites - there will be people who like roleplaying on the Latin or Greek sides of the Schism because those align with clear identity divides. But other than that, people will be interested in different Rites for the different mechanics they provide. Like, if Rites mostly had different stances on theological stances that didn't affect gameplay, most people aren't going to feel drawn to engage with the system. Rites that provide gameplay differences like concubinage/raiding/primogeniture will hook the player in to the religious gameplay in a way that helps with roleplay - they want to be able to be a fierce warlord and remain in good standing with the church, and those things are in conflict with each other unless they can align with a Rite that indulges them. And even then, there's a worry that their Rite could be declared a heresy, and that's a hook into entering Church politics to protect it! If the Rites don't affect much that's relevant to gameplay as a secular ruler, then there's just not much of an incentive to engage with the Church politics and instead players will just go without whatever Rite is local to their region or with the Pope, or whatever. Also, the clergy marriage example you give is compelling because of what it buffs: the DLC is adding theocratic ruler play, but unless you can have legitimate children as that theocratic ruler you're going to have to crash back into the secular branch of your dynasty on succession. So if you want to do a run as a dynasty of archbishops, you'll need to get yourself a Rite that allows for it. I think that's just fine, even if it does mean players are doing it for the stats/power/unlocks rather than because they're roleplaying about having a strong belief on whether it's proper for a priest to marry.

## Reply 491 — participant_075 · 2026-04-15 · post-31232534

There could be two options. Firstly, you could have of nepote mechanic, where you can poach heirs from your current dynasty (or bastard children of your own), and push them into roles within the church. This could involve founding a new house and adopting them into it. Should you die as bishop, you are succeeded by one of these individuals. Failing that you could to being a landed member of your dynasty (should they still exist) after your bishop character passes away. Of course, there should also be the option, like when victorious in crusades, that when you elevate a member of your dynasty to an ecclesiastical title, you get to play as them.

## Reply 499 — participant_275 · 2026-04-16 · post-31234369

I've said it before, but I'll say it again: the relationship between clerical and dynastic gameplay should generally be something like the early-modern Wittelsbachs, where there's a senior clerical title that the dynasty has some degree of influence over. Get your dynast appointed to the clerical title, and you can play as it; but the core succession play remains with the hereditary titles. By playing as (say) an archbishop, you're relinquishing some degree of control over the electoral duchy (or whatever) that got you that appointment in the first place. Want to do it again as your nephew in the next generation? Better hope that your brother/cousin/whoever co-operates in securing that nomination! (And also that he has enough heirs that he can spare one to become a celibate cleric.) nd

## Reply 509 — participant_278 · 2026-04-19 · post-31239781

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Videogames said: Daimyo issuing their own law codes like that is a Sengoku-era phenomenon, outside the scope of the CK3 timeline. But, I did post earlier about how the new mechanics could be used to better represent the medieval Japanese situation: Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
Good point on the Bunkoku-ho. However, the core of medieval Japan (within the CK3 timeline) was the coexistence of multiple overlapping legal systems: Kuge-ho, Buke-ho, Jisha-ho, and Honjo-ho. Even though Japan was a single religious sphere, "crimes" were defined by which legal framework was applied, and it was the secular authority that decided which system to use depending on the situation (the person or land involved). These laws also varied in scale: while Buke-ho acted as a relatively unified code for the warrior class, Honjo-ho was strictly local and applied only within specific estates. I hope the new system allows secular lords to choose or prioritize these legal layers depending on the context. Applying the logic of the new "Rite" system to secular law (as Secular Edicts) would perfectly represent regions where secular power dictates which rules apply, regardless of whether something is a religious "sin" or not. 公家法 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org 武家法 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org 寺院法 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org 本所法 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org 明法道 - Wikipedia ja.wikipedia.org
